Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.</span> <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">January 2019</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The pre-operative holding area<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[9]</a></sup> is so important in the surgical phase since here is where most of the family members can see who the staff of the surgery will be, also this area is where the nurses in charge to give information to the family members of the patient. In the pre-operative holding area, the person preparing for surgery changes out of his or her street clothes and is asked to confirm the details of his or her surgery. A set of vital signs are recorded, a peripheral <a href="/wiki/Intravenous_therapy" title="Intravenous therapy">IV line</a> is placed, and pre-operative medications (antibiotics, sedatives, etc.) are given.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[10]</a></sup> When the person enters the operating room, the skin surface to be operated on, called the operating field, is cleaned and prepared by applying an <a href="/wiki/Antiseptic" title="Antiseptic">antiseptic</a> (ideally <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chlorhexidine_gluconate" title="Chlorhexidine gluconate">chlorhexidine gluconate</a> in alcohol, as this is twice as effective as <a href="/wiki/Povidone-iodine" title="Povidone-iodine">povidone-iodine</a> at reducing the risk of infection).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[11]</a></sup> If hair is present at the surgical site, it is clipped off prior to prep application. The person is assisted by an anesthesiologist or resident to make a specific <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Surgical_Positions" title="Surgical Positions">surgical position</a>, then sterile drapes are used to cover the surgical site or at least a wide area surrounding the operating field; the drapes are clipped to a pair of poles near the head of the bed to form an "ether screen", which separates the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anesthetist" title="Anesthetist">anesthetist</a>/<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anesthesiologist" title="Anesthesiologist">anesthesiologist</a>'s working area (unsterile) from the surgical site (sterile).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Anesthesia" title="Anesthesia">Anesthesia</a> is administered to prevent <a href="/wiki/Pain" title="Pain">pain</a> from an incision, tissue manipulation and suturing.  Depending on the kind of operation, anesthesia may be provided <a href="/wiki/Local_anesthesia" title="Local anesthesia">locally</a> or as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/General_anesthesia" title="General anesthesia">general anesthesia</a>.  <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spinal_anesthesia" title="Spinal anesthesia">Spinal anesthesia</a> may be used when the surgical site is too large or deep for a local block, but general anesthesia may not be desirable.  With local and spinal anesthesia, the surgical site is anesthetized, but the person can remain conscious or minimally sedated.  In contrast, general anesthesia renders the person unconscious and paralyzed during surgery.  The person is <a href="/wiki/Intubation" title="Intubation">intubated</a> and is placed on a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mechanical_ventilator" title="Mechanical ventilator">mechanical ventilator</a>, and anesthesia is produced by a combination of injected and inhaled agents.
Choice of surgical method and <a href="/wiki/Anesthesia" title="Anesthesia">anesthetic</a> technique aims to reduce the risk of complications, shorten the time needed for recovery and minimise the <a href="/wiki/Surgical_stress" title="Surgical stress">surgical stress</a> response.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Intraoperative_phase">Intraoperative phase</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Surgery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Intraoperative phase">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The intraoperative phase begins when the surgery subject is received in the surgical area (such as the <a href="/wiki/Operating_theater" title="Operating theater">operating theater</a> or surgical <a href="/wiki/Hospital#Departments_or_wards" title="Hospital">department</a>), and lasts until the subject is transferred to a recovery area (such as a <a href="/wiki/Post-anesthesia_care_unit" title="Post-anesthesia care unit">post-anesthesia care unit</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p>An incision is made to access the surgical site.  <a href="/wiki/Blood_vessel" title="Blood vessel">Blood vessels</a> may be clamped or <a href="/wiki/Cauterization" title="Cauterization">cauterized</a> to prevent bleeding, and retractors may be used to expose the site or keep the incision open.  The approach to the surgical site may involve several layers of incision and dissection, as in abdominal surgery, where the incision must traverse skin, subcutaneous tissue, three layers of muscle and then the peritoneum.  In certain cases, <a href="/wiki/Bone" title="Bone">bone</a> may be cut to further access the interior of the body; for example, cutting the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_skull" title="Human skull">skull</a> for <a href="/wiki/Brain" title="Brain">brain</a> surgery or cutting the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_sternum" title="Human sternum">sternum</a> for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thoracic_surgery" title="Thoracic surgery">thoracic (chest) surgery</a> to open up the <a href="/wiki/Rib_cage" title="Rib cage">rib cage</a>. Whilst in surgery <a href="/wiki/Asepsis" title="Asepsis">aseptic technique</a> is used to prevent infection or further spreading of the disease. The surgeons' and assistants' hands, wrists and forearms are washed thoroughly for at least 4 minutes to prevent germs getting into the operative field, then sterile gloves are placed onto their hands. An antiseptic solution is applied to the area of the person's body that will be operated on. Sterile drapes are placed around the operative site. Surgical masks are worn by the surgical team to avoid germs on droplets of liquid from their mouths and noses from contaminating the operative site.
</p><p>Work to correct the problem in body then proceeds.  This work may involve:
<span class="anchor" id="excision"></span>
</p>
<ul><li>excision – cutting out an organ, tumor,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[14]</a></sup> or other tissue.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Segmental_resection" title="Segmental resection">resection</a> – partial removal of an organ or other bodily structure.</li>
<li>reconnection of organs, tissues, etc., particularly if severed.  Resection of organs such as intestines involves reconnection.  Internal <a href="/wiki/Surgical_suture" title="Surgical suture">suturing</a> or stapling may be used.  Surgical connection between blood vessels or other tubular or hollow structures such as loops of intestine is called <a href="/wiki/Anastomosis" title="Anastomosis">anastomosis</a>.</li>
<li>reduction – the movement or realignment of a body part to its normal position.  e.g. Reduction of a broken nose involves the physical manipulation of the bone or cartilage from their displaced state back to their original position to restore normal airflow and aesthetics.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ligature_(medicine)" title="Ligature (medicine)">ligation</a> – tying off blood vessels, ducts, or "tubes".</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medical_grafting" title="Medical grafting">grafts</a> – may be severed pieces of tissue cut from the same (or different) body or flaps of tissue still partly connected to the body but resewn for rearranging or restructuring of the area of the body in question.  Although grafting is often used in cosmetic surgery, it is also used in other surgery.  Grafts may be taken from one area of the person's body and inserted to another area of the body.  An example is <a href="/wiki/Vascular_bypass" title="Vascular bypass">bypass surgery</a>, where clogged blood vessels are bypassed with a graft from another part of the body.  Alternatively, grafts may be from other persons, cadavers, or animals.</li>
<li>insertion of <a href="/wiki/Prosthesis" title="Prosthesis">prosthetic</a> parts when needed.  Pins or screws to set and hold bones may be used.  Sections of bone may be replaced with prosthetic rods or other parts.  Sometimes a plate is inserted to replace a damaged area of skull.  <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Artificial_hip" title="Artificial hip">Artificial hip</a> replacement has become more common.  <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heart_pacemaker" title="Heart pacemaker">Heart pacemakers</a> or <a href="/wiki/Heart_valve" title="Heart valve">valves</a> may be inserted.  Many other types of <a href="/wiki/Prosthesis" title="Prosthesis">prostheses</a> are used.</li>
<li>creation of a <a href="/wiki/Stoma_(medicine)" title="Stoma (medicine)">stoma</a>, a permanent or semi-permanent opening in the body</li>
<li>in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organ_transplant" title="Organ transplant">transplant</a> surgery, the donor organ (taken out of the donor's body) is inserted into the recipient's body and reconnected to the recipient in all necessary ways (blood vessels, ducts, etc.).</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Arthrodesis" title="Arthrodesis">arthrodesis</a> – surgical connection of adjacent bones so the bones can grow together into one. <a href="/wiki/Spinal_fusion" title="Spinal fusion">Spinal fusion</a> is an example of adjacent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vertebrae" title="Vertebrae">vertebrae</a> connected allowing them to grow together into one piece.</li>
<li>modifying the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Digestive_tract" title="Digestive tract">digestive tract</a> in <a href="/wiki/Bariatric_surgery" title="Bariatric surgery">bariatric surgery</a> for <a href="/wiki/Weight_loss" title="Weight loss">weight loss</a>.</li>
<li>repair of a <a href="/wiki/Fistula" title="Fistula">fistula</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hernia" title="Hernia">hernia</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Prolapse" title="Prolapse">prolapse</a>.</li>
<li>repair according to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ICD-10-PCS" title="ICD-10-PCS">ICD-10-PCS</a>, in the Medical and Surgical Section 0, root operation Q, means restoring, to the extent possible, a body part to its normal anatomic structure and function. This definition, repair, is used only when the method used to accomplish the repair is not one of the other root operations. Examples would be <a href="/wiki/Colostomy" title="Colostomy">colostomy</a> takedown, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Herniorrhaphy" title="Herniorrhaphy">herniorrhaphy</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Hernia" title="Hernia">hernia</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Surgical_suture" title="Surgical suture">surgical suture</a> of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Laceration" title="Laceration">laceration</a>.</li>
<li>other procedures, including:</li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>clearing clogged ducts, blood or other vessels</li>
<li>removal of calculi (stones)</li>
<li>draining of accumulated fluids</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Debridement" title="Debridement">debridement</a> – removal of dead, damaged, or diseased tissue</li></ul></dd></dl>
<p><a href="/wiki/Blood_transfusion" title="Blood transfusion">Blood</a> or blood expanders may be administered to compensate for blood lost during surgery.  Once the procedure is complete, <a href="/wiki/Surgical_suture" title="Surgical suture">sutures</a> or <a href="/wiki/Surgical_staple" title="Surgical staple">staples</a> are used to close the incision.  Once the incision is closed, the anesthetic agents are stopped or reversed, and the person is taken off ventilation and <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/extubate" title="wikt:extubate">extubated</a> (if general anesthesia was administered).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-books.google.com_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.com-16">[15]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Postoperative_care">Postoperative care</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Surgery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Postoperative care">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After completion of surgery, the person is transferred to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Post_anesthesia_care_unit" title="Post anesthesia care unit">post anesthesia care unit</a> and closely monitored.  When the person is judged to have recovered from the anesthesia, he/she is either transferred to a surgical ward elsewhere in the hospital or discharged home.  During the post-operative period, the person's general function is assessed, the outcome of the procedure is assessed, and the surgical site is checked for signs of infection. There are several risk factors associated with postoperative complications, such as immune deficiency and obesity. Obesity has long been considered a risk factor for adverse post-surgical outcomes. It has been linked to many disorders such as obesity <a href="/wiki/Hypoventilation" title="Hypoventilation">hypoventilation</a> syndrome, <a href="/wiki/Atelectasis" title="Atelectasis">atelectasis</a> and pulmonary embolism, adverse cardiovascular effects, and wound healing complications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[16]</a></sup> If removable skin closures are used, they are removed after 7 to 10 days post-operatively, or after healing of the incision is well under way.
</p><p>It is not uncommon for <a href="/wiki/Drain_(surgery)" title="Drain (surgery)">surgical drains</a> to be required to remove blood or fluid from the surgical wound during recovery. Mostly these drains stay in until the volume tapers off, then they are removed.  These drains can become clogged, leading to <a href="/wiki/Abscess" title="Abscess">abscess</a>.
</p><p>Postoperative therapy may include <a href="/wiki/Adjuvant" title="Adjuvant">adjuvant</a> treatment such as <a href="/wiki/Chemotherapy" title="Chemotherapy">chemotherapy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Radiation_therapy" title="Radiation therapy">radiation therapy</a>, or administration of <a href="/wiki/Medication" title="Medication">medication</a> such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anti-rejection_medication" title="Anti-rejection medication">anti-rejection medication</a> for transplants. For postoperative nausea and vomiting (PONV), solutions like saline, water, controlled breathing placebo and aromatherapy can be used in addition to medication.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[17]</a></sup> Other follow-up studies or <a href="/wiki/Physical_therapy" title="Physical therapy">rehabilitation</a> may be prescribed during and after the recovery period. A recent post-operative care philosophy has been early ambulation. Ambulation is getting the patient moving around. This can be as simple as sitting up or even walking around. The goal is to get the patient moving as early as possible. It has been found to shorten the patient's length of stay. Length of stay is the amount of time a patient spends in the hospital after surgery before they are discharged. In a recent study<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[18]</a></sup> done with lumbar decompressions, the patient's length of stay was decreased by 1–3 days.
</p><p>The use of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antibacterial" title="Antibacterial">topical antibiotics</a> on surgical wounds to reduce infection rates has been questioned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AADfive_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-AADfive-20">[19]</a></sup> Antibiotic ointments are likely to irritate the skin, slow healing, and could increase risk of developing <a href="/wiki/Contact_dermatitis" title="Contact dermatitis">contact dermatitis</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antibiotic_resistance" title="Antibiotic resistance">antibiotic resistance</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AADfive_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-AADfive-20">[19]</a></sup> It has also been suggested that topical antibiotics should only be used when a person shows signs of infection and not as a preventative.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AADfive_20-2"><a href="#cite_note-AADfive-20">[19]</a></sup> A systematic review published by <a href="/wiki/Cochrane_(organisation)" title="Cochrane (organisation)">Cochrane (organisation)</a> in 2016, though, concluded that topical antibiotics applied over certain types of surgical wounds reduce the risk of surgical site infections, when compared to no treatment or use of <a href="/wiki/Antiseptic" title="Antiseptic">antiseptics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[20]</a></sup> The review also did not find conclusive evidence to suggest that topical antibiotics increased the risk of local skin reactions or antibiotic resistance.
</p><p>Through a retrospective analysis of national administrative data, the association between mortality and day of elective surgical procedure suggests a higher risk in procedures carried out later in the working week and on weekends. The odds of death were 44% and 82% higher respectively when comparing procedures on a Friday to a weekend procedure. This "weekday effect" has been postulated to be from several factors including poorer availability of services on a weekend, and also, decrease number and level of experience over a weekend.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>Postoperative pain affects an estimated 80% of people who underwent surgery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-23">[22]</a></sup> While pain is expected after surgery, there is growing evidence that pain may be inadequately treated in many people in the acute period immediately after surgery. It has been reported that incidence of inadequately controlled pain after surgery ranged from 25.1% to 78.4% across all surgical disciplines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[23]</a></sup> There is insufficient evidence to determine if giving opioid pain medication pre-emptively (before surgery) reduces postoperative pain the amount of medication needed after surgery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-23">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>Postoperative recovery has been defined as an energy‐requiring process to decrease physical symptoms, reach a level of emotional well‐being, regain functions, and re‐establish activities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[24]</a></sup> Moreover, it has been identified that patients who have undergone surgery are often not fully recovered on discharge.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Epidemiology">Epidemiology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Surgery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Epidemiology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">United States</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Surgery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: United States">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 2011, of the 38.6 million hospital stays in U.S. hospitals, 29% included at least one operating room procedure. These stays accounted for 48% of the total $387 billion in hospital costs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>The overall number of procedures remained stable from 2001 to 2011. In 2011, over 15 million operating room procedures were performed in U.S. hospitals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>Data from 2003 to 2011 showed that U.S. hospital costs were highest for the surgical service line; the surgical service line costs were $17,600 in 2003 and projected to be $22,500 in 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[27]</a></sup> For hospital stays in 2012 in the United States, private insurance had the highest percentage of surgical expenditure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moore_B,_Levit_K_and_Elixhauser_A_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Moore_B,_Levit_K_and_Elixhauser_A-29">[28]</a></sup> in 2012, mean hospital costs in the United States were highest for surgical stays.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moore_B,_Levit_K_and_Elixhauser_A_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-Moore_B,_Levit_K_and_Elixhauser_A-29">[28]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Special_populations">Special populations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Surgery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Special populations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Elderly_people">Elderly people</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Surgery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Elderly people">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Older adults have widely varying physical health.  <a href="/wiki/Frailty_syndrome" title="Frailty syndrome">Frail elderly</a> people are at significant risk of post-surgical complications and the need for extended care.  Assessment of older people before elective surgery can accurately predict the person's recovery trajectories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Frailty_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Frailty-30">[29]</a></sup> One frailty scale uses five items:  unintentional weight loss, <a href="/wiki/Muscle_weakness" title="Muscle weakness">muscle weakness</a>, exhaustion, low physical activity, and slowed walking speed.  A healthy person scores 0; a very frail person scores 5.  Compared to non-frail elderly people, people with intermediate frailty scores (2 or 3) are twice as likely to have post-surgical complications, spend 50% more time in the hospital, and are three times as likely to be discharged to a skilled nursing facility instead of to their own homes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Frailty_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-Frailty-30">[29]</a></sup> People who are frail and elderly (score of 4 or 5) have even worse outcomes, with the risk of being discharged to a nursing home rising to twenty times the rate for non-frail elderly people.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Children">Children</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Surgery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Children">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Surgery on children requires considerations that are not common in adult surgery. Children and adolescents are still developing physically and mentally making it difficult for them to make informed decisions and give consent for surgical treatments. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bariatric_Surgery#Youth" title="Bariatric Surgery">Bariatric surgery in youth</a> is among the controversial topics related to surgery in children.
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Pediatric_surgery" title="Pediatric surgery">Pediatric surgery</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pediatric_plastic_surgery" title="Pediatric plastic surgery">Pediatric plastic surgery</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vulnerable_populations">Vulnerable populations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Surgery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Vulnerable populations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Doctors perform surgery with the consent of the person undergoing surgery. Some people are able to give better <a href="/wiki/Informed_consent" title="Informed consent">informed consent</a> than others. Populations such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Incarceration" title="Incarceration">incarcerated persons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dementia" title="Dementia">people living with dementia</a>, the mentally incompetent, persons subject to coercion, and other people who are not able to make decisions with the same authority as others, have special needs when making decisions about their personal healthcare, including surgery.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Global_surgery">Global surgery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Surgery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Global surgery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Global surgery has been defined as 'the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Multidisciplinary" title="Multidisciplinary">multidisciplinary</a> enterprise of providing improved and equitable surgical care to the world's population, with its core belief as the issues of need, access and quality. <a href="/wiki/Halfdan_T._Mahler" title="Halfdan T. Mahler">Halfdan T. Mahler</a>, the 3rd Director-General of <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">the World Health Organization (WHO)</a>, first brought attention to the disparities in surgery and surgical care in 1980 when he stated in his address to the World Congress of the International College of Surgeons, "'the vast majority of the world's population has no access whatsoever to skilled surgical care and little is being done to find a solution."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[30]</a></sup> As such, surgical care globally has been described as the 'neglected stepchild of global health,' a term coined by <a href="/wiki/Paul_Farmer" title="Paul Farmer">Dr. Paul Farmer</a> to highlight the urgent need for further work in this area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[31]</a></sup> Furthermore, <a href="/wiki/Jim_Yong_Kim" title="Jim Yong Kim">Jim Young Kim</a>, the former President of the <a href="/wiki/World_Bank" title="World Bank">World Bank</a>, proclaimed in 2014 that "surgery is an indivisible, indispensable part of health care and of progress towards universal health coverage."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2015, the Lancet Commission on Global Surgery (LCoGS) published the landmark report titled "Global Surgery 2030: evidence and solutions for achieving health, welfare, and economic development," describing the large, pre-existing burden of surgical diseases in low- and middle-income countries (LMICs) and future directions for increasing universal access to safe surgery by the year 2030.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-34">[33]</a></sup> The Commission highlighted that about 5 billion people lack access to safe and affordable surgical and anesthesia care and 143 million additional procedures were needed every year to prevent further <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Morbidity" title="Morbidity">morbidity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mortality_rate" title="Mortality rate">mortality</a> from treatable surgical conditions as well as a $12.3 trillion loss in economic productivity by the year 2030.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-34">[33]</a></sup> This was especially true in the poorest countries, which account for over one-third of the population but only 3.5% of all surgeries that occur worldwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[34]</a></sup> It emphasized the need to significantly improve the capacity for Bellwether procedures – <a href="/wiki/Laparotomy" title="Laparotomy">laparotomy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Caesarean_section" title="Caesarean section">caesarean section</a>, <a href="/wiki/Open_fracture" title="Open fracture">open fracture care</a> – which are considered a minimum level of care that first-level hospitals should be able to provide in order to capture the most basic emergency surgical care.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_34-2"><a href="#cite_note-:2-34">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[35]</a></sup> In terms of the financial impact on the patients, the lack of adequate surgical and anesthesia care has resulted in 33 million individuals every year facing catastrophic health expenditure – the out-of-pocket healthcare cost exceeding 40% of a given household's income.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_34-3"><a href="#cite_note-:2-34">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>In alignment with the LCoGS call for action, the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Assembly" title="World Health Assembly">World Health Assembly</a> adopted the resolution WHA68.15 in 2015 that stated, "Strengthening emergency and essential surgical care and anesthesia as a component of universal health coverage."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-World_Health_Assembly_Resolution_WH_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-World_Health_Assembly_Resolution_WH-38">[37]</a></sup> This not only mandated the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">WHO</a> to prioritize strengthening the surgical and anesthesia care globally, but also led to governments of the member states recognizing the urgent need for increasing capacity in surgery and anesthesia. Additionally, the third edition of <a href="/wiki/Disease_Control_Priorities_Project" title="Disease Control Priorities Project">Disease Control Priorities</a> (DCP3), published in 2015 by the <a href="/wiki/World_Bank" title="World Bank">World Bank</a>, declared surgery as essential and featured an entire volume dedicated to building surgical capacity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p>Data from WHO and the World Bank indicate that scaling up infrastructure to enable access to surgical care in regions where it is currently limited or is non-existent is a low-cost measure relative to the significant morbidity and mortality caused by lack of surgical treatment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-40">[39]</a></sup> In fact, a systematic review found that the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cost-effectiveness" title="Cost-effectiveness">cost-effectiveness ratio</a> – dollars spent per DALYs averted – for surgical interventions is on par or exceeds those of major public health interventions such as <a href="/wiki/Oral_rehydration_therapy" title="Oral rehydration therapy">oral rehydration therapy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Breastfeeding_promotion" title="Breastfeeding promotion">breastfeeding promotion</a>, and even <a href="/wiki/Management_of_HIV/AIDS" title="Management of HIV/AIDS">HIV/AIDS antiretroviral therapy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[40]</a></sup> This finding challenged the common misconception that surgical care is financially prohibitive endeavor not worth pursuing in LMICs.
</p><p>A key policy framework that arose from this renewed global commitment towards surgical care worldwide is the National Surgical Obstetric and Anesthesia Plan (NSOAP).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-:4-42">[41]</a></sup> NSOAP focuses on policy-to-action capacity building for surgical care with tangible steps as follows: (1) analysis of baseline indicators, (2) partnership with local champions, (3) broad stakeholder engagement, (4) consensus building and synthesis of ideas, (5) language refinement, (6) costing, (7) dissemination, and (8) implementation. This approach has been widely adopted and has served as guiding principles between international collaborators and local institutions and governments. Successful implementations have allowed for sustainability in terms of longterm monitoring, quality improvement, and continued political and financial support.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-:4-42">[41]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_rights">Human rights</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Surgery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Human rights">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Access to surgical care is increasingly recognized as an integral aspect of healthcare, and therefore is evolving into a normative derivation of human <a href="/wiki/Right_to_health" title="Right to health">right to health</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[42]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ICESCR" title="ICESCR">ICESCR</a> Article 12.1 and 12.2 define the human <a href="/wiki/Right_to_health" title="Right to health">right to health</a> as "the right of everyone to the enjoyment of the highest attainable standard of physical and mental health"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[43]</a></sup> In the August 2000, the UN <a href="/wiki/Committee_on_Economic,_Social_and_Cultural_Rights" title="Committee on Economic, Social and Cultural Rights">Committee on Economic, Social and Cultural Rights</a> (CESCR) interpreted this to mean "right to the enjoyment of a variety of facilities, goods, services, and conditions necessary for the realization of the highest attainable health".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Comment_No._14_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Comment_No._14-45">[44]</a></sup> Surgical care can be thereby viewed as a positive right – an entitlement to protective healthcare.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Comment_No._14_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-Comment_No._14-45">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>Woven through the International Human and Health Rights literature is the right to be free from surgical disease. The 1966 ICESCR Article 12.2a described the need for "provision for the reduction of the stillbirth-rate and of infant mortality and for the healthy development of the child"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[45]</a></sup> which was subsequently interpreted to mean "requiring measures to improve… emergency obstetric services".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Comment_No._14_45-2"><a href="#cite_note-Comment_No._14-45">[44]</a></sup> Article 12.2d of the ICESCR stipulates the need for "the creation of conditions which would assure to all medical service and medical attention in the event of sickness",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[46]</a></sup> and is interpreted in the 2000 comment to include timely access to "basic preventative, curative services… for appropriate treatment of <a href="/wiki/Injury" title="Injury">injury</a> and <a href="/wiki/Disability" title="Disability">disability</a>.".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cultural_Rights_2000_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cultural_Rights_2000-48">[47]</a></sup> Obstetric care shares close ties with <a href="/wiki/Reproductive_rights" title="Reproductive rights">reproductive rights</a>, which includes access to reproductive health.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cultural_Rights_2000_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cultural_Rights_2000-48">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>Surgeons and <a href="/wiki/Public_health" title="Public health">public health</a> advocates, such as <a href="/wiki/Kelly_McQueen" title="Kelly McQueen">Kelly McQueen</a>, have described surgery as "Integral to the <a href="/wiki/Right_to_health" title="Right to health">right to health</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[48]</a></sup> This is reflected in the establishment of the WHO <a href="/wiki/Global_Initiative_for_Emergency_and_Essential_Surgical_Care" title="Global Initiative for Emergency and Essential Surgical Care">Global Initiative for Emergency and Essential Surgical Care</a> in 2005,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[49]</a></sup> the 2013 formation of the Lancet Commission for Global Surgery,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[50]</a></sup> the 2015 <a href="/wiki/World_Bank" title="World Bank">World Bank</a> Publication of Volume 1 of its <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Disease_Control_Priorities&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Disease Control Priorities (page does not exist)">Disease Control Priorities</a> "Essential Surgery",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[51]</a></sup> and the 2015 <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Assembly" title="World Health Assembly">World Health Assembly</a> 68.15 passing of the Resolution for Strengthening Emergency and Essential Surgical Care and <a href="/wiki/Anesthesia" title="Anesthesia">Anesthesia</a> as a Component of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Universal_Health_Coverage" title="Universal Health Coverage">Universal Health Coverage</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-World_Health_Assembly_Resolution_WH_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-World_Health_Assembly_Resolution_WH-38">[37]</a></sup> The Lancet Commission for Global Surgery outlined the need for access to "available, affordable, timely and safe" surgical and anesthesia care;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[52]</a></sup> dimensions paralleled in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ICESCR" title="ICESCR">ICESCR</a> General Comment No. 14, which similarly outlines need for available, accessible, affordable and timely healthcare.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Comment_No._14_45-3"><a href="#cite_note-Comment_No._14-45">[44]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Surgery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/History_of_surgery" title="History of surgery">History of surgery</a>, <a href="/wiki/Prehistoric_medicine" title="Prehistoric medicine">Prehistoric medicine</a>, and <a href="/wiki/History_of_general_anesthesia" title="History of general anesthesia">History of general anesthesia</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Edwin_Smith_Papyrus_v2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1954" data-file-width="2550" decoding="async" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Edwin_Smith_Papyrus_v2.jpg/220px-Edwin_Smith_Papyrus_v2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Edwin_Smith_Papyrus_v2.jpg/330px-Edwin_Smith_Papyrus_v2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Edwin_Smith_Papyrus_v2.jpg/440px-Edwin_Smith_Papyrus_v2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Edwin_Smith_Papyrus_v2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Plates vi &amp; vii of the <a href="/wiki/Edwin_Smith_Papyrus" title="Edwin Smith Papyrus">Edwin Smith Papyrus</a>, an Egyptian surgical treatise</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Trepanation">Trepanation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Surgery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Trepanation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Surgical treatments date back to the prehistoric era. The oldest for which there is evidence is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trepanation" title="Trepanation">trepanation</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[53]</a></sup> in which a hole is <a href="/wiki/Drill" title="Drill">drilled</a> or scraped into the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_skull" title="Human skull">skull</a>, thus exposing the <a href="/wiki/Dura_mater" title="Dura mater">dura mater</a> in order to treat health problems related to intracranial pressure and other diseases.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_Egypt">Ancient Egypt</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Surgery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Ancient Egypt">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Prehistoric surgical techniques are seen in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Ancient Egypt</a>, where a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_mandible" title="Human mandible">mandible</a> dated to approximately 2650 BC shows two perforations just below the root of the first <a href="/wiki/Molar_(tooth)" title="Molar (tooth)">molar</a>, indicating the draining of an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tooth_abscess" title="Tooth abscess">abscessed tooth</a>. Surgical texts from ancient Egypt date back about 3500 years ago. Surgical operations were performed by priests, specialized in medical treatments similar to today,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cossurg_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-cossurg-55">[54]</a></sup> and used sutures to close wounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[55]</a></sup> Infections were treated with honey.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="India">India</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Surgery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: India">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Remains from the early Harappan periods of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indus_Valley_civilization" title="Indus Valley civilization">Indus Valley civilization</a> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 3300 BC</span>) show evidence of teeth having been drilled dating back 9,000 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[57]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Sushruta_Samhita" title="Sushruta Samhita">Sushruta Samhita</a></i> is one of the oldest known surgical texts and its period is usually placed around 1200–600 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-singhguide_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-singhguide-59">[58]</a></sup> It describes in detail the examination, diagnosis, treatment, and prognosis of numerous ailments, as well as procedures for various forms of cosmetic surgery, <a href="/wiki/Plastic_surgery" title="Plastic surgery">plastic surgery</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rhinoplasty" title="Rhinoplasty">rhinoplasty</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[59]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_and_Medieval_Greece">Ancient and Medieval Greece</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Surgery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Ancient and Medieval Greece">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:162px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hippocrates_rubens.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3301" data-file-width="2365" decoding="async" height="223" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Hippocrates_rubens.jpg/160px-Hippocrates_rubens.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Hippocrates_rubens.jpg/240px-Hippocrates_rubens.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Hippocrates_rubens.jpg/320px-Hippocrates_rubens.jpg 2x" width="160"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hippocrates_rubens.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Hippocrates" title="Hippocrates">Hippocrates</a> stated in the oath (c. 400 BC) that general physicians must never practice surgery and that surgical procedures are to be conducted by specialists</div></div></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">ancient Greece</a>, temples dedicated to the healer-god <a href="/wiki/Asclepius" title="Asclepius">Asclepius</a>, known as <i>Asclepieia</i> (<a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>: <span lang="el">Ασκληπιεία</span>, sing. <i>Asclepieion</i> <i>Ασκληπιείον</i>), functioned as centers of medical advice, prognosis, and healing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[60]</a></sup> In the Asclepieion of <a href="/wiki/Epidaurus" title="Epidaurus">Epidaurus</a>, some of the surgical cures listed, such as the opening of an abdominal abscess or the removal of traumatic foreign material, are realistic enough to have taken place.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-books.google.com_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.com-16">[15]</a></sup> The Greek <a href="/wiki/Galen" title="Galen">Galen</a> was one of the greatest surgeons of the ancient world and performed many audacious operations – including brain and eye surgery – that were not tried again for almost two millennia.
</p><p>Researchers from the <a href="/wiki/Adelphi_University" title="Adelphi University">Adelphi University</a> discovered in the Paliokastro on <a href="/wiki/Thasos" title="Thasos">Thasos</a> ten skeletal remains, four women and six men, who were buried between the fourth and seventh centuries A.D. Their bones illuminated their physical activities, traumas, and even a complex form of brain surgery. According to the researchers: "The very serious trauma cases sustained by both males and females had been treated surgically or orthopedically by a very experienced physician/surgeon with great training in trauma care. We believe it to have been a military physician". The researchers were impressed by the complexity of the brain surgical operation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1991 at the Polystylon fort in Greece, researchers discovered the head of a Byzantine warrior of the 14th century. Analysis of the lower jaw revealed that a surgery has been performed, when the warrior was alive, to the jaw which had been badly fractured and it tied back together until it healed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[62]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Islamic_world">Islamic world</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Surgery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Islamic world">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Golden_Age" title="Islamic Golden Age">Islamic Golden Age</a>, largely based upon <a href="/wiki/Paul_of_Aegina" title="Paul of Aegina">Paul of Aegina</a>'s <i>Pragmateia</i>, the writings of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abulcasis" title="Abulcasis">Abulcasis</a> (Abu al-Qasim Khalaf ibn al-Abbas Al-Zahrawi), an <a href="/wiki/Al-Andalus" title="Al-Andalus">Andalusian-Arab</a> physician and scientist who practiced in the Zahra suburb of <a href="/wiki/C%C3%B3rdoba,_Spain" title="Córdoba, Spain">Córdoba</a>, were influential.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[64]</a></sup> Al-Zahrawi specialized in curing disease by <a href="/wiki/Cauterization" title="Cauterization">cauterization</a>. He invented several <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Surgical_instruments" title="Surgical instruments">surgical instruments</a> for purposes such as inspection of the interior of the <a href="/wiki/Urethra" title="Urethra">urethra</a> and for removing foreign bodies from the throat, the ear, and other body organs. He was also the first to illustrate the various <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cannulae" title="Cannulae">cannulae</a> and to treat <a href="/wiki/Wart" title="Wart">warts</a> with an iron tube and caustic metal<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (August 2019)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> as a boring instrument. He describes what is thought to be the first attempt at reduction <a href="/wiki/Mammaplasty" title="Mammaplasty">mammaplasty</a> for the management of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gynaecomastia" title="Gynaecomastia">gynaecomastia</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anzjournal_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Anzjournal-66">[65]</a></sup> and the first <a href="/wiki/Mastectomy" title="Mastectomy">mastectomy</a> to treat <a href="/wiki/Breast_cancer" title="Breast cancer">breast cancer</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aramco0816_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-aramco0816-67">[66]</a></sup> He is credited with the performance of the first <a href="/wiki/Thyroidectomy" title="Thyroidectomy">thyroidectomy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[67]</a></sup> Al-Zahrawi pioneered techniques of <a href="/wiki/Neurosurgery" title="Neurosurgery">neurosurgery</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neurological" title="Neurological">neurological</a> diagnosis, treating <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Head_injuries" title="Head injuries">head injuries</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Skull_fractures" title="Skull fractures">skull fractures</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spinal_injuries" title="Spinal injuries">spinal injuries</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydrocephalus" title="Hydrocephalus">hydrocephalus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Subdural_effusion" title="Subdural effusion">subdural effusions</a> and <a href="/wiki/Headache" title="Headache">headache</a>. The first clinical description of an operative procedure for <a href="/wiki/Hydrocephalus" title="Hydrocephalus">hydrocephalus</a> was given by Al-Zahrawi, who clearly describes the evacuation of superficial <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intracranial" title="Intracranial">intracranial</a> fluid in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrocephalic" title="Hydrocephalic">hydrocephalic</a> children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[68]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_modern_Europe">Early modern Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Surgery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Early modern Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:162px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ambroise_Par%C3%A9_1573.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="571" data-file-width="400" decoding="async" height="228" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Ambroise_Par%C3%A9_1573.jpg/160px-Ambroise_Par%C3%A9_1573.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Ambroise_Par%C3%A9_1573.jpg/240px-Ambroise_Par%C3%A9_1573.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Ambroise_Par%C3%A9_1573.jpg/320px-Ambroise_Par%C3%A9_1573.jpg 2x" width="160"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ambroise_Par%C3%A9_1573.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ambroise Paré (c. 1510–1590), father of modern military surgery.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Augenoperation_1195.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1534" data-file-width="1073" decoding="async" height="214" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Augenoperation_1195.jpg/150px-Augenoperation_1195.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Augenoperation_1195.jpg/225px-Augenoperation_1195.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Augenoperation_1195.jpg/300px-Augenoperation_1195.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Augenoperation_1195.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>12th century eye surgery in Italy</div></div></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>, the demand grew for surgeons to formally study for many years before practicing; universities such as <a href="/wiki/University_of_Montpellier" title="University of Montpellier">Montpellier</a>, <a href="/wiki/University_of_Padua" title="University of Padua">Padua</a> and <a href="/wiki/University_of_Bologna" title="University of Bologna">Bologna</a> were particularly renowned. In the 12th century, <a href="/wiki/Rogerius_(physician)" title="Rogerius (physician)">Rogerius Salernitanus</a> composed his <i>Chirurgia</i>, laying the foundation for modern Western surgical manuals. <a href="/wiki/Barber_surgeon" title="Barber surgeon">Barber-surgeons</a> generally had a bad reputation that was not to improve until the development of academic surgery as a specialty of medicine, rather than an accessory field.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[69]</a></sup> Basic surgical principles for asepsis etc., are known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Halsteads_principles" title="Halsteads principles">Halsteads principles</a>.
</p><p>There were some important advances to the art of surgery during this period. The professor of anatomy at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Padua" title="University of Padua">University of Padua</a>, <a href="/wiki/Andreas_Vesalius" title="Andreas Vesalius">Andreas Vesalius</a>, was a pivotal figure in the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> transition from classical medicine and anatomy based on the works of <a href="/wiki/Galen" title="Galen">Galen</a>, to an empirical approach of 'hands-on' dissection. In his anatomic treaties <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/De_humani_corporis_fabrica" title="De humani corporis fabrica">De humani corporis fabrica</a></i>, he exposed the many anatomical errors in Galen and advocated that all surgeons should train by engaging in practical dissections themselves.
</p><p>The second figure of importance in this era was <a href="/wiki/Ambroise_Par%C3%A9" title="Ambroise Paré">Ambroise Paré</a> (sometimes spelled "Ambrose"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[70]</a></sup>), a French army surgeon from the 1530s until his death in 1590. The practice for cauterizing gunshot wounds on the battlefield had been to use boiling oil; an extremely dangerous and painful procedure. Paré began to employ a less irritating emollient, made of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Egg_yolk" title="Egg yolk">egg yolk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rose_oil" title="Rose oil">rose oil</a> and <a href="/wiki/Turpentine" title="Turpentine">turpentine</a>. He also described more efficient techniques for the effective <a href="/wiki/Ligature_(medicine)" title="Ligature (medicine)">ligation</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Blood_vessel" title="Blood vessel">blood vessels</a> during an <a href="/wiki/Amputation" title="Amputation">amputation</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_surgery">Modern surgery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Surgery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Modern surgery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The discipline of surgery was put on a sound, scientific footing during the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Age of Enlightenment</a> in Europe. An important figure in this regard was the Scottish surgical scientist, <a href="/wiki/John_Hunter_(surgeon)" title="John Hunter (surgeon)">John Hunter</a>, generally regarded as the father of modern scientific surgery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[71]</a></sup> He brought an <a href="/wiki/Empiricism" title="Empiricism">empirical</a> and <a href="/wiki/Experiment" title="Experiment">experimental</a> approach to the science and was renowned around Europe for the quality of his research and his written works. Hunter reconstructed surgical knowledge from scratch; refusing to rely on the testimonies of others, he conducted his own surgical experiments to determine the truth of the matter. To aid comparative analysis, he built up a collection of over 13,000 specimens of separate organ systems, from the simplest plants and animals to humans.
</p><p>He greatly advanced knowledge of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Venereal_disease" title="Venereal disease">venereal disease</a> and introduced many new techniques of surgery, including new methods for repairing damage to the <a href="/wiki/Achilles_tendon" title="Achilles tendon">Achilles tendon</a> and a more effective method for applying ligature of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arteries" title="Arteries">arteries</a> in case of an <a href="/wiki/Aneurysm" title="Aneurysm">aneurysm</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[72]</a></sup> He was also one of the first to understand the importance of <a href="/wiki/Pathology" title="Pathology">pathology</a>, the danger of the spread of <a href="/wiki/Infection" title="Infection">infection</a> and how the problem of <a href="/wiki/Inflammation" title="Inflammation">inflammation</a> of the wound, bone <a href="/wiki/Lesion" title="Lesion">lesions</a> and even <a href="/wiki/Tuberculosis" title="Tuberculosis">tuberculosis</a> often undid any benefit that was gained from the intervention. He consequently adopted the position that all surgical procedures should be used only as a last resort.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p>Other important 18th- and early 19th-century surgeons included <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Percival_Pott" title="Percival Pott">Percival Pott</a> (1713–1788) who described <a href="/wiki/Pott_disease" title="Pott disease">tuberculosis on the spine</a> and first demonstrated that a cancer may be caused by an environmental <a href="/wiki/Carcinogen" title="Carcinogen">carcinogen</a> (he noticed a connection between <a href="/wiki/Chimney_sweep" title="Chimney sweep">chimney sweep</a>'s exposure to soot and their high incidence of <a href="/wiki/Chimney_sweeps%27_carcinoma" title="Chimney sweeps' carcinoma">scrotal cancer</a>). <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astley_Paston_Cooper" title="Astley Paston Cooper">Astley Paston Cooper</a> (1768–1841) first performed a successful ligation of the abdominal aorta, and <a href="/wiki/James_Syme" title="James Syme">James Syme</a> (1799–1870) pioneered the Symes Amputation for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ankle_joint" title="Ankle joint">ankle joint</a> and successfully carried out the first <a href="/wiki/Hemipelvectomy" title="Hemipelvectomy">hip disarticulation</a>.
</p><p>Modern <a href="/wiki/Pain" title="Pain">pain</a> control through <a href="/wiki/Anesthesia" title="Anesthesia">anesthesia</a> was discovered in the mid-19th century. Before the advent of <a href="/wiki/Anesthesia" title="Anesthesia">anesthesia</a>, surgery was a traumatically painful procedure and surgeons were encouraged to be as swift as possible to minimize patient <a href="/wiki/Suffering" title="Suffering">suffering</a>.  This also meant that operations were largely restricted to <a href="/wiki/Amputation" title="Amputation">amputations</a> and external growth removals. Beginning in the 1840s, surgery began to change dramatically in character with the discovery of effective and practical anaesthetic chemicals such as <a href="/wiki/Diethyl_ether" title="Diethyl ether">ether</a>, first used by the American surgeon <a href="/wiki/Crawford_Long" title="Crawford Long">Crawford Long</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Chloroform" title="Chloroform">chloroform</a>, discovered by Scottish obstetrician <a href="/wiki/James_Young_Simpson" title="James Young Simpson">James Young Simpson</a> and later pioneered by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_Snow_(physician)" title="John Snow (physician)">John Snow</a>, physician to <a href="/wiki/Queen_Victoria" title="Queen Victoria">Queen Victoria</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[74]</a></sup> In addition to relieving patient suffering, anaesthesia allowed more intricate operations in the internal regions of the human body.  In addition, the discovery of <a href="/wiki/Muscle_relaxant" title="Muscle relaxant">muscle relaxants</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Curare" title="Curare">curare</a> allowed for safer applications.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Infection_and_antisepsis">Infection and antisepsis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Surgery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Infection and antisepsis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Unfortunately, the introduction of anesthetics encouraged more surgery, which inadvertently caused more dangerous patient post-operative infections.  The concept of infection was unknown until relatively modern times. The first progress in combating infection was made in 1847 by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hungarian_people" title="Hungarian people">Hungarian</a> doctor <a href="/wiki/Ignaz_Semmelweis" title="Ignaz Semmelweis">Ignaz Semmelweis</a> who noticed that medical students fresh from the dissecting room were causing excess maternal death compared to midwives. Semmelweis, despite ridicule and opposition, introduced compulsory handwashing for everyone entering the maternal wards and was rewarded with a plunge in maternal and fetal deaths; however, the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society" title="Royal Society">Royal Society</a> dismissed his advice.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Joseph_Lister2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="980" data-file-width="709" decoding="async" height="235" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Joseph_Lister2.jpg/170px-Joseph_Lister2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Joseph_Lister2.jpg/255px-Joseph_Lister2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Joseph_Lister2.jpg/340px-Joseph_Lister2.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Joseph_Lister2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Joseph_Lister,_1st_Baron_Lister" title="Joseph Lister, 1st Baron Lister">Joseph Lister</a>, pioneer of <a href="/wiki/Antiseptic#Usage_in_surgery" title="Antiseptic">antiseptic surgery</a></div></div></div>
<p>Until the pioneering work of British surgeon <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Joseph_Lister,_1st_Baron_Lister" title="Joseph Lister, 1st Baron Lister">Joseph Lister</a> in the 1860s, most medical men believed that chemical damage from exposures to bad air (see "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Miasma_theory_of_disease" title="Miasma theory of disease">miasma</a>") was responsible for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Infections" title="Infections">infections</a> in wounds, and facilities for washing hands or a patient's <a href="/wiki/Wound" title="Wound">wounds</a> were not available.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-storyof_medicine_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-storyof_medicine-76">[75]</a></sup> Lister became aware of the work of French <a href="/wiki/Chemist" title="Chemist">chemist</a> <a href="/wiki/Louis_Pasteur" title="Louis Pasteur">Louis Pasteur</a>, who showed that rotting and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fermentation_(food)" title="Fermentation (food)">fermentation</a> could occur under <a href="/wiki/Anaerobic_infection" title="Anaerobic infection">anaerobic conditions</a> if <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Micro-organisms" title="Micro-organisms">micro-organisms</a> were present.  Pasteur suggested three methods to eliminate the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Micro-organisms" title="Micro-organisms">micro-organisms</a> responsible for <a href="/wiki/Gangrene" title="Gangrene">gangrene</a>: filtration, exposure to heat, or exposure to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chemical_solutions" title="Chemical solutions">chemical solutions</a>. Lister confirmed Pasteur's conclusions with his own experiments and decided to use his findings to develop <a href="/wiki/Antiseptic" title="Antiseptic">antiseptic</a> techniques for wounds.  As the first two methods suggested by Pasteur were inappropriate for the treatment of human tissue, Lister experimented with the third, spraying <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbolic_acid" title="Carbolic acid">carbolic acid</a> on his instruments. He found that this remarkably reduced the incidence of gangrene and he published his results in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Lancet" title="The Lancet">The Lancet</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[76]</a></sup> Later, on 9 August 1867, he read a paper before the British Medical Association in Dublin, on the <i><a href="/wiki/Antiseptic_Principle_of_the_Practice_of_Surgery" title="Antiseptic Principle of the Practice of Surgery">Antiseptic Principle of the Practice of Surgery</a></i>, which was reprinted in the <i>British Medical Journal</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FUSourcebook_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-FUSourcebook-79">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PG-JL_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-PG-JL-80">[79]</a></sup> His work was groundbreaking and laid the foundations for a rapid advance in infection control that saw modern antiseptic operating theatres widely used within 50 years.
</p><p>Lister continued to develop improved methods of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antisepsis" title="Antisepsis">antisepsis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Asepsis" title="Asepsis">asepsis</a> when he realised that infection could be better avoided by preventing bacteria from getting into wounds in the first place. This led to the rise of sterile surgery. Lister introduced the Steam Steriliser to <a href="/wiki/Sterilization_(microbiology)" title="Sterilization (microbiology)">sterilize</a> equipment, instituted rigorous hand washing and later implemented the wearing of rubber gloves. These three crucial advances – the adoption of a scientific methodology toward surgical operations, the use of anaesthetic and the introduction of sterilised equipment – laid the groundwork for the modern invasive surgical techniques of today.
</p><p>The use of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/X-rays" title="X-rays">X-rays</a> as an important medical diagnostic tool began with their discovery in 1895 by German <a href="/wiki/Physicist" title="Physicist">physicist</a> <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_R%C3%B6ntgen" title="Wilhelm Röntgen">Wilhelm Röntgen</a>. He noticed that these rays could penetrate the skin, allowing the skeletal structure to be captured on a specially treated <a href="/wiki/Photographic_plate" title="Photographic plate">photographic plate</a>.
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 235px"><div style="width: 235px">

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Surgery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist reflist-lower-alpha">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-1">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">From the <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>: <span lang="el">χειρουργική</span> <i>cheirourgikē</i> (composed of χείρ, "hand", and ἔργον, "work"), via <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">Latin</a>: <i lang="la">chirurgiae</i>, meaning "hand work".</span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>

<li>Lei, Y. T., Xie, J. W., Huang, Q., Huang, W., &amp; Pei, F. X. (2021). Benefits of early   ambulation within 24 h after total knee arthroplasty: a multicenter retrospective cohort study in China. Military Medical Research, 8(1), 17. https://doi.org/10.1186/s40779-021-00310-x</li>
<li>Stethen, T. W., Ghazi, Y. A., Heidel, R. E., Daley, B. J., Barnes, L., Patterson, D., &amp;   McLoughlin, J. M. (2018).
